class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D61555%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DHongshun%2BZhao%252C%2BYanli%2BQi%252C%2BKang%2BLiang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D13%26issueNum%3D51%26contentID%3Dacsami.1c20154%26title%3DInterface-Driven%2BPseudocapacitance%2BEndowing%2BSandwiched%2BCoSe2%252FN-Doped%2BCarbon%252FTiO2%2BMicrocubes%2Bwith%2BUltra-Stable%2BSodium%2BStorage%2Band%2BLong-Term%2BCycling%2BStability%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D61564%26publicationDate%3DDecember%2B2021" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/medium/am1c20154_0009.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/large/am1c20154_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c20154&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Cobalt diselenide (CoSe<sub>2</sub>) has drawn great concern as an anode material for sodium-ion batteries due to its considerable theoretical capacity. Nevertheless, the poor cycling stability and rate performance still impede its practical implantation. Here, CoSe<sub>2</sub>/nitrogen-doped carbon-skeleton hybrid microcubes with a TiO<sub>2</sub> layer (denoted as TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>) are favorably prepared via a facile template-engaged strategy, in which a TiO<sub>2</sub>-coated Prussian blue analogue of Co<sub>3</sub>[Co(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> is used as a new precursor accompanied with a selenization procedure. Such structures can concurrently boost ion and electron diffusion kinetics and inhibit the structural degradation during cycling through the close contact between the TiO<sub>2</sub> layer and NC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>. Besides, this hybrid structure promotes the superior Na-ion intercalation pseudocapacitance due to the well-designed interfaces. The as-prepared TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> microcubes exhibit a superior cycling capability (511 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles) and long cycling life (456 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 6.4 A g<sup>–1</sup> for 6000 cycles with a retention of 92.7%). Coupled with a sodium vanadium fluorophosphate (Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>)@C cathode, this assembled full cell displays a specific capacity of 281 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> for 100 cycles. This work can be potentially used to improve other metal selenide-based anodes for rechargeable batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D61555%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DHongshun%2BZhao%252C%2BYanli%2BQi%252C%2BKang%2BLiang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D13%26issueNum%3D51%26contentID%3Dacsami.1c20154%26title%3DInterface-Driven%2BPseudocapacitance%2BEndowing%2BSandwiched%2BCoSe2%252FN-Doped%2BCarbon%252FTiO2%2BMicrocubes%2Bwith%2BUltra-Stable%2BSodium%2BStorage%2Band%2BLong-Term%2BCycling%2BStability%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D61564%26publicationDate%3DDecember%2B2021" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291445&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Chemical structure" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Chemical%20structure" class="article__tags__link">Chemical structure</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291557&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Coating materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Coating%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Coating materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292200&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Layers" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Layers" class="article__tags__link">Layers</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290689&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Oxides" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Oxides" class="article__tags__link">Oxides</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cose2&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_CoSe2">CoSe<sub>2</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Tio2+Coating&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_TiO2%20coating">TiO<sub>2</sub> coating</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Pseudocapacitance&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_pseudocapacitance">pseudocapacitance</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Full+Cell&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20full%20cell">sodium-ion full cell</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) possessing similar structure characteristics and electrochemical principles as lithium-ion batteries have drawn enormous attention on account of cheaper and earth abundance of sodium resources. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> Nonetheless, the larger radius and sluggish diffusion kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> hinder its further development and industrial application of SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 4 to 6">(4−6)</a> As a result, numerous efforts have been devoted to figure out proper high-storage anode materials for SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 7 and 8">(7,8)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Recently, transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs) represent a series of potential anode materials on the basis of the conversion mechanism. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 9 to 15">(9−15)</a> Among them, being abundant and eco-friendly, CoSe<sub>2</sub> has been proved for valid sodium storage performance with a small band gap, easily controlled morphology, and superior theoretical capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 16 to 19">(16−19)</a> Unfortunately, it still suffers from rapid capacity loss even at a low current rate and unstable electrode–electrolyte interface. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20,21)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">To overcome these challenges and take advantage of the host talent of CoSe<sub>2</sub>, multiple efforts have been proposed. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 7 and 22 to 26">(7,22−26)</a> As reported, with a rational construction of nanostructures, not only can the reaction kinetics of the electrodes be improved but also the vulnerability incurred by large volume expansion can be alleviated. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref27" aria-label="reference 16 and 27">(16,27)</a> For example, Cui et al. found that CoSe<sub>2</sub> nanofibers delivered a remarkable electrochemical performance (371.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>/200 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after 500 cycles). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> Nevertheless, nanoparticles are frequently susceptible to agglomeration during cycling, thus resulting in rapid capacity fading and unqualified budget needs. Therefore, to deal with this issue, designing micro–nanostructures is an efficient strategy. Furthermore, compositing with carbon materials such as graphene <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 29 and 30">(29,30)</a> and carbon nanotubes <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 31 and 32">(31,32)</a> is also a valuable technology. Among them, the carbon layer doped with heteroatoms depicts superior conductivity. Specially, the nitrogen-doped carbon layer can fruitfully generate defects and improve material’s conductivity, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref24 ref33 ref34 ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref24 ref33 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 4, 24 and 33 to 35">(4,24,33−35)</a> bringing about enhanced energy storage performance. Prussian blue analogue (PBA), a nitrogen-rich carbon-based material, could constitute a conductive network after heat treatment, which can significantly improve the conductivity, thus facilitating the electrode material’s electrochemical performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref30" aria-label="reference 27 and 30">(27,30)</a> On the other hand, as we all know, TiO<sub>2</sub> is a promising anode with appealing characteristics, including cheap cost, good structural robustness, and negligible volume change due to its layered crystal structure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref21 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref21 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 9, 21 and 36 to 39">(9,21,36−39)</a> In comparison with conventional conductive coatings, it owns redox-active properties. For instance, Fu et al. fabricated α-Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>(Ferric oxide)@TiO<sub>2</sub> core–shell nanospheres that showed an outstanding capacity of 267 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at around 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> over 300 cycles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> Hence, it is necessary to take advantage of TiO<sub>2</sub> as the coating layer to relieve volume changes of TMDs and provide a highway for charge transfer. Last but not least, pseudocapacitive charge storage has also been proven to be a very effective tactics for SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3 ref40 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 3, 40 and 41">(3,40,41)</a> Therefore, this synergy between the traditional sodium storage and pseudocapacitance process can confirm a remarkably high-rate property. However, as far as we know, this method of TiO<sub>2</sub>-coated CoSe<sub>2</sub> confined in PBA-derived nitrogen-doped carbon microboxes has not been applied for SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this study, we illustrate an elaborate anode for the CoSe<sub>2</sub> microparticles confined in an N-doped carbon skeleton with a TiO<sub>2</sub> layer (TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>). When assessed as an anode for SIBs, the as-prepared TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> displays a remarkable reversible capacity (511 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>), exceptional rate property (464 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 6.4 A g<sup>–1</sup>), and ultra-long cyclability (456 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 6.4 A g<sup>–1</sup> for 6000 cycles with a retention of 92.7%). More significantly, TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> demonstrates favorable electrochemical performance in sodium-ion full cells when coupled with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>@C as the cathode. This full cell depicts a reversible capacity of 323.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Such improved Na storage performance could be attributed to many aspects: (1) the CoSe<sub>2</sub> microcubes and N-doped carbon skeleton both promote electron transfer and offer easy access to Na<sup>+</sup> and (2) the coated TiO<sub>2</sub> layer could accommodate variation in the volume of CoSe<sub>2</sub> and also prevent it from pulverizing and aggregating.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Synthesis of Co–Co PBA Microcubes</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Co–Co PBA microcubes were prepared via a reported procedure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> In a typical synthetic procedure, 79.7 mg of cobalt acetate was added into 40 mL of deionized water to obtain solution A and 66.4 mg of potassium hexacyanocobaltate and 1.4 g of sodium lauryl sulfate were added into 40 mL of deionized water to prepare solution B. After that, solution A was slowly added to solution B and mixed thoroughly. After stirring for 3 h, the mixture was shelved for 24 h at 25 °C. The precipitate was collected through centrifugation, washed with ethanol five times, and dried in an oven for 12 h at 60 °C.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Synthesis of Co–Co PBA@TiO<sub>2</sub> Microcubes</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The as-synthesized Co–Co PBA (0.2 g) was dispersed in absolute ethanol (60 mL) and added to the concentrated ammonia solution (0.15 mL, 28 wt %). After ultrasonic dispersion for 1 h, 100 μL of tetrabutyl titanate (TBOT) was added dropwise to the mixed solution at a rate of 1 mL min<sup>–1</sup>. In order to control the thickness of the TiO<sub>2</sub> shell, the amount of TBOT ranged from 100 to 300 μL. After magnetically stirring for 20 h at room temperature, the products were collected by centrifugation, washed with ethanol five times, and dried in an oven for 8 h at 70 °C.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Synthesis of TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> Microcubes</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Firstly, 200 mg of Co–Co PBA@TiO<sub>2</sub> and 800 mg of Se powder (Aladdin) were ground uniformly and dispersed in a ceramic boat. Subsequently, the mixture was heated in an Ar/H<sub>2</sub> (95:5 in volume) atmosphere at 350 °C and maintained for around 4 h. The as-prepared black powder is the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>. For comparison, NC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> without a TiO<sub>2</sub> coating was synthesized via a similar synthetic procedure on a mixture of Co–Co PBA particles and Se powder.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The synthesis process of TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> microcubes is schematically demonstrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>. In detail, Co–Co PBA microcube precursors are obtained via a traditional precipitation process. Subsequently, they are coated with a thin layer of TiO<sub>2</sub> on the surface through a sol–gel method. Then, the as-acquired Co–Co PBA@TiO<sub>2</sub> core–shell microcubes are uniformly ground with Se powder and annealed, resulting in synchronous selenization and carbonization. As expected, the final product retains its pristine cubic structure. This unique structure could heighten conductivity and ion diffusion, which affords high-performance anode materials in SIBs.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/medium/am1c20154_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/large/am1c20154_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic illustration of the synthetic procedure of TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> microcubes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/large/am1c20154_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c20154&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of both samples were acquired. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a, the XRD patterns can be favorably assigned to standard cubic CoSe<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS # 65-3327). This may be due to the thin coating layer or amorphous phase of the TiO<sub>2</sub> shell. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> Raman spectra were also conducted to further analyze CoSe<sub>2</sub>, TiO<sub>2</sub>, and carbon materials in composite materials. The two weak peaks in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b at 401 and 520 cm<sup>–1</sup> are typical vibration modes of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>, conveying the satisfactory combination of CoSe<sub>2</sub> and TiO<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> Meanwhile, a distinct peak (≈194 cm<sup>–1</sup>) is derived from the A<sub>g</sub><sup>1</sup> mode of the out-of-plane oscillation of the Co–Se band, and another (≈239 cm<sup>–1</sup>) is attached to the E<sub>2g</sub><sup>1</sup> manner of the in-plane vibration of the Co–Se band (as shown in the inset), also verifying that the CoSe<sub>2</sub> was successfully prepared. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref42" aria-label="reference 7 and 42">(7,42)</a> Compared with NC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>, the abovementioned corresponding peaks have slightly shifted, which could be ascribed to the possible surface strain resulting from the TiO<sub>2</sub> shell. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> Besides, the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> electrode shows a higher A<sub>g</sub><sup>1</sup>/E<sub>2g</sub><sup>1</sup> intensity ratio, indicating that it has more active edge sites, which can store even extra Na ions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a> Moreover, the D peaks (≈1336 cm<sup>–1</sup>) and G peaks (≈1571 cm<sup>–1</sup>) are results from the sp<sup>3</sup> and sp<sup>2</sup> hybrid carbon intensity ratio (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) of approximately 1.04, verifying rather high graphite properties of the nitrogen-doped carbon coating. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> Specifically, the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> of TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> is higher than that of NC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> samples, demonstrating that its carbon component has more defects and disordered structures. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref30" aria-label="reference 20 and 30">(20,30)</a> TGA curves in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c demonstrate the thermal behavior of the two materials, where similar curves can be observed. As for NC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>, it shows two-step weight loss processes, which originate from the combustion of carbon and the decomposition of CoSe<sub>2</sub> into Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, while for TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>, concretely, the initial weight loss appearing at around 380 °C results from the transformation of CoSe<sub>2</sub> to CoSeO<sub>4</sub> and SeO<sub>2</sub>. After that, the subsequent weight loss may be because of the inflammation of carbon materials and the conversion of CoSeO<sub>4</sub> to Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> at 500 °C. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref32 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32 ref45" aria-label="reference 31, 32 and 45">(31,32,45)</a> A rough verification about the mass content of CoSe<sub>2</sub> is 78.86%, and the detailed calculating process is added in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/suppl_file/am1c20154_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Note S1</a>.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/medium/am1c20154_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/large/am1c20154_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) XRD patterns, (b) Raman spectra, (c) TGA curves, and (d) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherm of TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> and NC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/large/am1c20154_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c20154&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Furthermore, N<sub>2</sub> absorption/desorption isotherms in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/suppl_file/am1c20154_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S1</a> indicate that the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller surface areas of NC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> and TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> are 14.18 and 23.52 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The enlarged surface area might provide sufficient Na<sup>+</sup> insertion sites and enhance the pseudocapacitance contribution, resulting in improved electrochemical performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The morphology and microstructure of TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> micro–nano-heterostructures were collected via scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). The TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> (shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a–c) typically maintained the cubic appearance and measurement of the Co–Co PBA precursor (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/suppl_file/am1c20154_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>) after TiO<sub>2</sub> coating and calcination, whereas the surface became rougher with assembled particles, demonstrating the favorable TiO<sub>2</sub> coating. Besides, the dented surface of these cube particles is ascribed to the disintegration of precursor core and selenization process, which causes a shrinkage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> Observe carefully the edge of the particles (as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d); there is an amorphous shell structure of approximately 4 nm, which should be a conformal TiO<sub>2</sub> coating. The typical TEM image in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e shows two distinct lattice fringes with a spacing of 0.26 and 0.23 nm, separately, in accordance with the (210) and (120) lattice planes of CoSe<sub>2</sub>, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref36" aria-label="reference 24 and 36">(24,36)</a> Meanwhile, a set of lattice fringes around 0.35 nm could be inspected, highly consistent with the (101) plane of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref36" aria-label="reference 21 and 36">(21,36)</a> The selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f) displays numerous diffraction rings, verifying the constitution of a polycrystalline structure. The X-ray energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mappings (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>g) show equal layouts of C, N, Co, and Se elements, while Ti is mostly restricted on the exterior, suggesting that this well-designed architecture cleverly integrates the characteristics of the hybrid micro–nanostructures.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/medium/am1c20154_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/large/am1c20154_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a–c) SEM and (d,e) HRTEM images, (f) corresponding SAED pattern, and (g) EDS mappings (scale: 500 nm) of TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/large/am1c20154_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c20154&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) spectra were deconvolved to examine the surface species of TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>. The existences of Ti, C, N, Co, and Se are detected in the XPS survey spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a), which are consistent with the EDS result. In addition, the quantitative data analysis shows that the atomic percentage ratio of Co and Se is 2.23 (4.38%/10.07%), demonstrating the formation of CoSe<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/suppl_file/am1c20154_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> The broad peak in the Ti 2p XPS spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b) can be divided into two major peaks at around 459.5 and 465.3 eV, related to Ti 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Ti 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46 ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 46 and 47">(46,47)</a> In terms of the C 1s XPS spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c), two peaks centered at 284.6 and 286.3 eV, attributing to the C–C sp<sup>2</sup> and N–C/C–C sp<sup>2</sup> groups, respectively. Besides, the appearance of the C–C peak reveals the construction of graphitic carbon, and the N–C bond suggests that N is well doped into this carbon structure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> Furthermore, the N 1s spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d) could be favorably assigned to three types of N peaks located at 398.9 (pyridine nitrogen), 399.9 (pyrrole nitrogen), and 400.8 eV (graphitic nitrogen). Among them, pyridine nitrogen and pyrrole nitrogen have been considered to improve electronical conductivity by facilitating the transport of electrons through the π-conjugated carbon structure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref27" aria-label="reference 9 and 27">(9,27)</a> What is more is that it can induce additional storage sites for Na<sup>+</sup>, enhance reaction kinetics, and provide an abundant active surface, thereby improving the overall electrochemical performance. Moreover, the peaks at 778.5 and 793.6 eV in the Co 2p pattern, depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e, represent the appearance of Co<sup>2+</sup> due to the partial reduction of CoSe<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>f, the binding energies of 55.10, 55.64, and 58.94 eV are ascribed to Co–Se of Se 3d<sub>5/2</sub>, Se–Se of Se 3d<sub>3/2</sub>, and Se–O–Se, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> The reason for the appearance of SeO<sub><i>x</i></sub> may be the surface oxidation caused by the testing process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/medium/am1c20154_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/large/am1c20154_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) XPS survey spectrum of TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>. High-resolution XPS spectra for TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>: (b) Ti 2p, (c) C 1s, (d) N 1s, (e) Co 2p, and (f) Se 3d.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/large/am1c20154_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c20154&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical property of the as-synthesized samples was evaluated by the galvanostatic charge–discharge process. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a illustrates the initial discharge–charge curves of the two electrodes at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The pristine TiO<sub>2</sub>, NC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>, and TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> anodes display the first charge–discharge reversible capacities of 195.9/388.9, 632.1/810.1, and 708.4/802.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with initial Coulombic efficiencies (ICE) of 50.26, 78.02, and 88.26%, respectively. The initial capacity loss is primarily caused by the formation of the SEI film and carbonaceous materials such as N-doped carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref46" aria-label="reference 29 and 46">(29,46)</a> Besides, the additional TiO<sub>2</sub> will consume Na to form the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TiO<sub>2</sub> compound. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> In the subsequent cycles, the platform in the first discharge plot is transformed into three platforms (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/suppl_file/am1c20154_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4</a>). In comparison with pristine TiO<sub>2</sub> and NC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>, the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> electrode depicts a tardy capacity loss in the cycling. Besides, it also depicts a higher ICE, conveying that a steadier SEI is established on the surface of the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> microcube. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 8 and 9">(8,9)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/medium/am1c20154_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/large/am1c20154_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Electrochemical performance of the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> electrode: (a) initial discharge–charge curves. (b) Typical discharge–charge voltage profiles. (c) Cycling performance at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> and (d) rate cycling behavior. (e) Discharge–charge voltage curves under different current densities. (f) Comparison of rate performances between TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> and the previously reported literature. (g) Long-term cycling performance at an extremely high current density of 6.4 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (h) Schematic illustration of the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>/C full cell. (i) Cycling performance at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> and (j) rate performance of this full cell at 0.2–5 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/large/am1c20154_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c20154&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Furthermore, the cycling stability of all samples is assessed at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c. It is obvious that TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> exhibits a higher specific capacity of 511.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> up to 200 cycles, and its capacity retention, calculated from the fourth cycle, is 81.95%, demonstrating that the well-conceived materials are not crushed in the subsequent cycle. According to the SEM image shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/suppl_file/am1c20154_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5b</a>, significant changes in the structure cannot be observed after cycling, where it still maintains a relatively complete cubic shape. In contrast, it is found that the capacity of the NC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> electrode decays rapidly after 30 cycles, and the capacity is close to 0 after 200 cycles, which means that the battery has completely failed. Indeed, the cubic structure of nanomaterials collapsed, and some nanoparticles have agglomerated, as affirmed by SEM images (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/suppl_file/am1c20154_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a</a>). During cycling, the outer TiO<sub>2</sub> shell can beneficially prevent the decay and mechanical failure of CoSe<sub>2</sub> electrodes, and it is also converted to Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TiO<sub>2</sub>, where Ti<sup>3+</sup> in Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TiO<sub>2</sub> is desired to improve the integrated conductivity of the electrode, resulting in an increase in capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref50" aria-label="reference 21 and 50">(21,50)</a> To further study the role of TiO<sub>2</sub> in the electrode, a controlled sample obtained by adjusting the TBOT content from 50 to 200 μL was evaluated as an anode for SIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/suppl_file/am1c20154_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). In the cyclic stability data of the electrodes, it can be obtained that the thicker TiO<sub>2</sub> coating layer will unavoidably reduce the capacity owing to the hindered transport of Na<sup>+</sup> into the internal.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d,e compares the rate performances. Irreversible capacity losses are apparently observed in the initial two cycles, which is most likely due to the decomposition of the electrolyte and/or solvent. Consistent with the abovementioned cycling performance, the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> performs better at high current densities than pristine TiO<sub>2</sub> and NC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>. As for TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>, the charge capacities of 682, 665, 648, 622, 595, and 528 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> are delivered at 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.4, 0.8, and 1.6 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Even at an ultrahigh rate of 3.2 and 6.4 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacities keep at 488 and 464 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, separately. Meanwhile, as the rate returns to 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the specific capacity also returns to 692 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. This phenomenon could be due to the reactivation course result from high-rate sodiation. The greatly improved high-rate performance is probably attributed to the formation of the TiO<sub>2</sub> layer via tiny particles, which offers a shorter diffusion path for electron/ion transport, better contact with the electrolyte, and internal space to reduce powdering strain. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Moreover, the long-cycling test at a rate of 6.4 A g<sup>–1</sup> is further studied (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g). The reversible capacity decreases to 457 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in the 6000th cycle and remains steady afterward with a capacity retention of over 92.73%. This result suggests that the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> anode, as expected, depicts a comprehensive effect combining the merits for each component. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/suppl_file/am1c20154_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>, in comparison with the literature on CoSe<sub>2</sub>-based anodes for SIBs, the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> displays impressive electrochemical performance. Hence, the TiO<sub>2</sub> core–shell structure not only relieves the pulverization and severe volume change of CoSe<sub>2</sub> microcubes but also provides additional interstitial sites and appropriate-sized pathways, which serves as a steady membrane for the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction courses, thereby boosting the reversible Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> A full cell (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>h) using the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> anode and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>/C cathode was assembled to demonstrate the practical use of the as-reported anode. Besides, the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>/C full cell has an N/P ratio of 1.168, and its average voltage is around 0.83 V. Additionally, the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>/C full cell (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>i) affords a reversible capacity of 281 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> and steady cycling after 100 cycles. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>j shows remarkable capacities of 344, 306, 278, 260, and 227 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at different rates of 0.2–5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Such results further certify that the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> electrode is a promising anode for SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">The ion transport kinetics of the two samples were further collected by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) curves at around 1.4 V (25 °C), and the relevant results are exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a. From <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/suppl_file/am1c20154_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S4</a>, after the first cycle, the <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> (3.142 Ω) and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> (69.15 Ω) of TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> are slightly higher than those of NC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> (2.807 and 62.86 Ω, respectively) due to the relatively low electronic conductivity of TiO<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> However, the robust structure of TiO<sub>2</sub> makes it slightly increase after 100 cycles. This may be due to the optimization of the SEI film structure and the progress of the cycle process; the CoSe<sub>2</sub> micro–nanoparticles gradually realize further structural nanometerization. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> On the contrary, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of the NC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> electrode increases significantly upon prolonged cycling, which could be attributed to the structural collapse of the samples. This fact also confirms the abovementioned SEM result. Additionally, according to <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/suppl_file/am1c20154_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Note S1</a>, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of the electrode is inversely proportional to σ, the slope of the line <i>Z</i>′ versus ω<sup>–1/2</sup> in the low-frequency region. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/suppl_file/am1c20154_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>, the TNC@CoSe<sub>2</sub> electrode has the minimum slope values, which represents the largest Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/medium/am1c20154_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/large/am1c20154_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Nyquist plots of TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> samples. Diffusion coefficients calculated from the GITT during (b) sodiation and (c) desodiation. (d) Initial three CV curves and (e) CVs at various scan rates of TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>. (f) Linear relationship of log(<i>v</i>) and log(<i>i</i>). (g) Pseudocapacitive contribution of the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> sample at 0.8 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (h) Percentage of pseudocapacitance contributions of the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> sample.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/large/am1c20154_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c20154&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Besides, this phenomenon demonstrates that these architecture networks can establish an effective bi-continuous pathway for both electron and Na<sup>+</sup> transport, thus ameliorating diffusion kinetics. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b,c shows the <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> obtained by the constant galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) test (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/suppl_file/am1c20154_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/suppl_file/am1c20154_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Note S3</a>), further verifying the enhanced Na insertion/extraction kinetics of TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>. Specifically, the <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> changes from 1.69 × 10<sup>–15</sup> to 7.93 × 10<sup>–14</sup> during the sodiation process, while the value varies from 1.51 × 10<sup>–14</sup> to 1.01 × 10<sup>–12</sup> during the desodiation process, displaying a higher <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> value, which is consistent with the EIS results. These results illustrate that this TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> architecture has superior stability, lower polarization, and faster Na<sup>+</sup> transfer, affirming its outstanding electrochemical property as a potential anode for SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">To investigate the sodiation/desodiation behavior, the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of the two electrodes are depicted in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/suppl_file/am1c20154_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S9</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d, respectively, and the curve shapes resemble those of the earlier reported CoSe<sub>2</sub> materials. With regard to TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>, in the first cycle, the broad cathodic peak at about 0.75 V corresponds to the initial sodium insertion process, which is likely to form an intermediate phase of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TiO<sub>2</sub>. Besides, a distinct cathodic peak centered at 0.804 V is ascribed to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq 1</a>, for the process of SEI formation, Na<sup>+</sup> insertion, and conversion reaction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> In the following cathodic scans, this peak becomes three peaks appearing at 1.396, 1.216, and 0.596 V, representing the transformation among CoSe<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoSe<sub>2</sub>, CoSe, and Co, respectively, which are given in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eqs 2">eqs 2</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45 ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref53" aria-label="reference 45 and 53">(45,53)</a> In terms of the first charge process, a peak at 1.962 V with a shoulder at 1.883 V relates to the recovery of CoSe<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals from metallic Co and Na<sub>2</sub>Se (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="eq 4">eq 4</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 31 and 32">(31,32)</a> Moreover, the CV curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>e) overlap quite fine from the second cycle, suggesting a favorable reversibility of the sodiation/desodiation process. Furthermore, the abovementioned charge–discharge profiles for the two electrodes are also shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/suppl_file/am1c20154_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4</a>. From the second discharge curve, there are three obvious voltage platforms emerging at 0.823, 1.251, and 1.457 V, resulting from the sodium reactions with TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>, which are consistent with the CV results. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/medium/am1c20154_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i8"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/medium/am1c20154_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i9"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/medium/am1c20154_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i10"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq4"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/medium/am1c20154_m004.gif" alt="" id="_i11"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></div></div></span></div><div class="NLM_p">Further analysis of the underlying causes for the superior rate performance and long-term cycling life of TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>, CV curves from 0.2 to 8.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, were conducted. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>e, all CV plots exhibit near-peak appearances and positions, demonstrating the weak polarization of the electrode. In fact, the whole stored charges were contributed by capacitance/pseudocapacitance or intercalation capacity. The relationship between the scan rate (<i>v</i>) and current density (<i>i</i>) described by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq5" aria-label="eqs 5">eqs 5</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq6" aria-label="6">6</a> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref27" aria-label="reference 7 and 27">(7,27)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq5"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/medium/am1c20154_m005.gif" alt="" id="_i12"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(5)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq6"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/medium/am1c20154_m006.gif" alt="" id="_i13"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(6)</span></div></div></span></div><div class="NLM_p">Several distinct cathodic (0.609 and 1.376 V)/anodic (2.046 V) peaks were selected to calculate the <i>b</i> values, which can be used to determine the Na storage mechanism. Generally, <i>b</i> = 1 denotes a capacitive process (i.e., pseudocapacitance when involving a Faradaic reaction), while <i>b</i> = 0.5 manifests a capacitance-led process. As calculated, the <i>b</i> values for the three peaks are 0.726, 0.907, and 0.838 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>f), signifying the partially capacitive control behavior. Moreover, <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq7" aria-label="eq 7">eq 7</a> was utilized to calculate the pseudocapacitance contribution <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref27" aria-label="reference 7 and 27">(7,27)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq7"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/medium/am1c20154_m007.gif" alt="" id="_i14"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(7)</span></div></div></span>in which <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>0.5</sup> correspond to the capacitive and diffusive capacity, respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p">At 0.8 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>g), the capacitance contribution accounts for 64.91% of the total capacity of TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>. Compared with NC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/suppl_file/am1c20154_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>), as the scan rate increases, the capacitance contribution reaches a maximum value of over 97.32% at 8 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>h). It is elaborated that pseudocapacitive behavior plays an important role in promoting the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption/desorption, and this influence is more evident at high current densities, in which the inner CoSe<sub>2</sub> microcubes deliver the intrinsic rate contribution, N-doped carbon serves as a conductive terrace, and the external TiO<sub>2</sub> can also offer interface sites for capacitive adsorptions. Meanwhile, this improved Na<sup>+</sup> reaction kinetic performance of the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> anode is further supported by the abovementioned EIS results.</div><div class="NLM_p">The Na storage mechanism of the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> electrode has been further studied by ex situ XRD (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a). When it is discharged to 1.15 V (stage C), the typical peak of CoSe appears on the XRD spectrum, and at 0.5 V (stage D), the formation of Co and Na<sub>2</sub>Se can be observed. At the state of 1.75 V (stage E), the peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>Se minute away and peaks of CoSe<sub>2</sub> appeared again at 2.8 V (stage H). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> The good reversibility of the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> electrode has also been confirmed by HRTEM and SAED (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b–e). For one thing, upon discharging further to 0.01 V, the ex situ HRTEM (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b) exhibits that the <i>d</i>-spacing of 0.246, 0.386, and 0.178 nm could be attributed to Na<sub>2</sub>Se(220), Na<sub>2</sub>Se(111), and Co(200), respectively, which can also be confirmed by the SAED pattern (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>c). For another, in the fully charge state, the Na<sub>2</sub>Se and Co phases completely disappear, while the CoSe<sub>2</sub> phase could be observed, demonstrating that the CoSe<sub>2</sub> phase experiences the course of thorough desodiation and return to its initial states. The ex situ HRTEM and SAED patterns (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>d,e) reveal consistent results with the ex situ XRD data.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/medium/am1c20154_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/large/am1c20154_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) Ex situ XRD patterns of the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> microcube electrode at different discharge–charge states with the corresponding discharge–charge curve. HRTEM images and SAED patterns of the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> microcube electrode discharged to 0.01 V (b,c) and charged to 2.9 V (d,e).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c20154/asset/images/large/am1c20154_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c20154&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The improved electrochemical performance has affirmed that the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> electrode will be a potential anode for SIBs. Many reasons account for this superior performance. First, the nitrogen-doped carbon framework can boost the interfacial electron transport, resulting in swift reaction kinetics. Second, the external TiO<sub>2</sub> serves as a buffer layer to adapt to the volume expansion of CoSe<sub>2</sub> because of its superior structure stability, which can be well proven by the morphology of the samples after cycling. Meantime, the 3D network crystal structure of TiO<sub>2</sub> provides empty zigzag channels to offer interstitial sites and appropriate-sized pathways for Na<sup>+</sup>. Last but not least, this hybrid structure promotes a strong pseudocapacitance charge storage, thus resulting in the high rate capability.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i15">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, a novel nanostructure for CoSe<sub>2</sub> micro–nanoparticles confined in a N-doped carbon framework with a TiO<sub>2</sub> coating (TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>) has been successfully synthesized. Benefiting from the special architecture and multicompositional characteristics, the TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> hybrid microcubes exhibit remarkable Na storage properties on the basis of an outstanding cycling capability (511 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles) and ultra-long cycling life (456 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 6.4 A g<sup>–1</sup> for 6000 cycles with a retention of 92.7%), contrary to the sharp capacity decreasing after 50 cycles of NC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> electrodes without a TiO<sub>2</sub> shell. Moreover, the sodium-ion full cell with TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> as the anode and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>/C as the cathode demonstrates the excellent cycling performance (281 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles). In addition, pseudocapacitive behavior associated with the surface/near-surface reactions dominates the Na storage property of the as-prepared TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> electrode. The present work demonstrates a potential anode material for SIBs and the effectiveness of complex multicompositional hybrids toward various applications.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i16">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c20154?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c20154</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Material characterizations; electrochemical measurements; N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherm of NC-CoSe<sub>2</sub>; SEM images of Co–Co PBA; TEM images of TNC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> microcubes; typical discharge–charge voltage profiles of NC-CoSe<sub>2</sub> and TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes; Soc. Rev.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>46</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3529</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3614</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/c6cs00776g</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D46%26publication_year%3D2017%26pages%3D3529-3614%26journal%3DChem.%2BSoc.%2BRev.%26author%3DJ.-Y.%2BHwang%26author%3DS.-T.%2BMyung%26author%3DY.-K.%2BSun%26title%3DSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%253A%2BPresent%2Band%2BFuture%26doi%3D10.1039%252Fc6cs00776g&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.1c20154&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2Fc6cs00776g&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGot-2IKXj-Q-7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg9YGpM4rhMpw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXkvF2rsbk%253D&amp;md5=cfc7b2d153696dcbded22d954ff06174</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1007/s40820-020-0391-9"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gaddam, R. R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Golberg, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, X. S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Stabilising Cobalt Sulphide Nanocapsules with Nitrogen-Doped Carbon for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Storage</span>. <i>Nano-Micro Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>12</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">48</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1007/s40820-020-0391-9</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D12%26publication_year%3D2020%26pages%3D48%26journal%3DNano-Micro%2BLett.%26author%3DY.%2BWu%26author%3DR.%2BR.%2BGaddam%26author%3DC.%2BZhang%26author%3DH.%2BLu%26author%3DC.%2BWang%26author%3DD.%2BGolberg%26author%3DX.%2BS.%2BZhao%26title%3DStabilising%2BCobalt%2BSulphide%2BNanocapsules%2Bwith%2BNitrogen-Doped%2BCarbon%2Bfor%2BHigh-Performance%2BSodium-Ion%2BStorage%26doi%3D10.1007%252Fs40820-020-0391-9&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.1c20154&amp;doiOfLink=10.1007%2Fs40820-020-0391-9&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Stabilising cobalt sulphide nanocapsules with nitrogen-doped carbon for high-performance sodium-ion storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Yilan; Gaddam, Rohit R.; Zhang, Chao; Lu, Hao; Wang, Chao; Golberg, Dmitri; Zhao, Xiu Song</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano-Micro Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">48</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NLAEBV</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2150-5551</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nano-Micro Letters</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Conversion-type anode materials with a high charge storage capability generally suffer from large vol. expansion, poor electron cond., and sluggish metal ion transport kinetics.  The electrode material described in this paper, namely cobalt sulfide nanoparticles encapsulated in carbon cages (Co9S8@NC), can circumvent these problems.  This electrode material exhibited a reversible sodium-ion storage capacity of 705 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1 with an extraordinary rate capability and good cycling stability.  Mechanistic study using the in situ transmission electron microscope technique revealed that the volumetric expansion of the Co9S8 nanoparticles is buffered by the carbon cages, enabling a stable electrode-electrolyte interface.  In addn., the carbon shell with high-content doped nitrogen significantly enhances the electron cond. of the Co9S8@ NC electrode material and provides doping-induced active sites to accommodate sodium ions.  By integrating the Co9S8@ NC as neg. electrode with a cellulose-derived porous hard carbon/graphene oxide composite as pos. electrode and 1 M NaPF6 in diglyme as the electrolyte, the sodium-ion capacitor full cell can achieve energy densities of 101.4 and 45.8 Wh kg-1 at power densities of 200 and 10,000 W kg-1, resp.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqnPOB01qJKIrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg1-GZviRVkXg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXhs1ehurc%253D&amp;md5=71036c1c7fd5465fedb8c9ec12d8f67a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1016/j.ensm.2017.12.014"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ali, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>;                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">CoPSe: A New Ternary Anode Material for Stable and High-Rate Sodium/Potassium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Feng, Yutong; Xu, Mengzhu; He, Ting; Chen, Bingjie; Gu, Feng; Zu, Lianhai; Meng, Ruijin; Yang, Jinhu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">33</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">16</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2007262</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADVMEW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0935-9648</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The exploration of ideal electrode materials overcoming the crit. problems of large electrode vol. changes and sluggish redox kinetics induced by large ionic radius of Na+/K+ ions is highly desirable for sodium/potassium-ion batteries (SIBs/PIBs) toward large-scale applications.  The present work demonstrates that single-phase ternary cobalt phosphoselenide (CoPSe) in the form of nanoparticles embedded in a layered metal-org. framework (MOF)-derived N-doped carbon matrix (CoPSe/NC) represents an ultrastable and high-rate anode material for SIBs/PIBs.  The CoPSe/NC is fabricated by using the MOF as both a template and precursor, coupled with in situ synchronous phosphorization/selenization reactions.  The CoPSe anode holds a set of intrinsic merits such as lower mech. stress, enhanced reaction kinetics, as well as higher theor. capacity and lower discharge voltage relative to its counterpart of CoSe2, and suppressed shuttle effect with higher intrinsic elec. cond. relative to CoPS.  The involved mechanisms are evidenced by substantial characterizations and d. functional theory (DFT) calcns.  Consequently, the CoPSe/NC anode shows an outstanding long-cycle stability and rate performance for SIBs and PIBs.  Moreover, the CoPSe/NC-based Na-ion full cell can achieve a higher energy d. of 274 Wh kg-1, surpassing that based on CoSe2/NC and most state-of-the-art Na-ion full cells based on P-, Se-, or S-contg. binary/ternary anodes to date.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrGdeOmnJzeJbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg1-GZviRVkXg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXmtFajt70%253D&amp;md5=4ac79e28321803529457a2016ae22dd4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1016/j.jmst.2020.10.045"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ye, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gong, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">P-Doped CoSe<sub>2</sub> Nanoparticles Embedded in 3D Honeycomb-Like Carbon Network for Long Cycle-Life Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.
